<introduction>
	<p>
		Before we can apply the Laplace transform to solve differential equations, we need to understand the fundamental concept that makes the method work: transferring derivatives inside an integral. This section begins with a review of integration by parts, illustrating how derivatives applied to one function can be shifted onto another. 
	</p>

	<p>
		Building on this idea, we define the Laplace transform as an integral that takes a function of <m>t</m> and produces a new function of <m>s</m>. Through detailed examples, we compute transforms of simple functions and highlight when these transforms exist. By the end of this section, you'll see how Laplace transforms simplify calculus problems into algebraic ones, and how this shift makes solving differential equations far more manageable.
	</p>
</introduction>

<subsection><title> Transferring Derivatives Inside an Integral </title>
	<lemma><title> üëÄ <em>Reading Preparation</em> </title>

		<p>
			<task label="prep-derivative-transfer-1"><title> Exponential Derivatives </title>
	
				<statement>
					<p>
						Match each exponential function with its derivative. Assume that <m>s</m> is a constant.
					</p>
				</statement>
		
				<cardsort>
					<match>
						<premise><m>  e^{2t} </m></premise>
						<response><m> 2e^{2t} </m></response>
					</match>
					<match>
						<premise><m>  e^{-4t} </m></premise>
						<response><m> -4e^{-4t} </m></response>
					</match>
					<match>
						<premise><m>  e^{\pi t} </m></premise>
						<response><m> \pi e^{\pi t} </m></response>
					</match>
					<match>
						<premise><m>  e^{st} </m></premise>
						<response><m> se^{st} </m></response>
					</match>
					<match>
						<premise><m>  e^{-st} </m></premise>
						<response><m> -se^{-st} </m></response>
					</match>
				</cardsort>
		
			</task>
	
			<task label="prep-derivative-transfer-2"><title> Integration by Parts </title>
				<statement>
					<p>
						Click on the correct integration by parts formula.
					</p>
				</statement>
				<areas>
					<p/>
					<p>
						<sidebyside margins="10% 5%">
							<p><area correct="no"> <m>\ds \int u dv = \int v du</m></area></p>
							<p><area correct="no"> <m>\ds \int u dv = uv + \int v du</m></area></p>
						</sidebyside>
					</p>
					<p>
						<sidebyside margins="10% 5%">
							<p><area correct="yes"><m>\ds \int u dv = uv - \int v du</m></area></p>
							<p><area correct="no"> <m>\ds \int u dv = udv - \int u dv</m></area></p>
						</sidebyside>
					</p>
				</areas>
			</task>
		</p>
	
	</lemma>

	<p>
		To understand how the Laplace transform method simplifies differential equations into algebraic equations, we must revisit integration by parts from calculus. The minimalistic version of the formula is:
		<me>
			\int u\,dv = uv - \int v\,du
		</me>
	</p>

	<p>
		If <m>u</m> and <m>v</m> are functions of <m>t</m> on <m>[a,b]</m>, then we can write a more detailed version that makes the transfer of derivatives clear:
		<me>
			\int_{a}^{b} \os{{\color{RoyalBlue}\large\text{derivative on }v}}{
			\us{u}{ \us{\uparrow}{\ul{ 
				u(t)
			}}} \ 
			\us{dv}{\us{\uparrow}{\ul{
				{\color{RoyalBlue}\os{\downarrow}{v'\vphantom{\Large\Gamma}}(t)} dt
			}}}
			}
			= \us{u}{\us{\uparrow}{\ul{ u(t) }}}\ \us{v}{\us{\uparrow}{\ul{ v(t) }}} \Big|_{a}^{b}
			- \int_{a}^{b} \os{{\color{RoyalBlue}\large\text{derivative on }u}}{
				\us{v}{ \us{\uparrow}{\ul{ v(t) }}} \ 
				\us{du}{\us{\uparrow}{\ul{ {\color{RoyalBlue}\os{\downarrow}{u'\vphantom{\Large\Gamma}}(t)} dt }}}
			}
		</me>
	</p>

	<p>
		This version shows how integration by parts lets you shift a derivative from one function to another inside an integral. That becomes especially useful when <m>u(t) = e^{-st}</m> for some constant <m>s</m>.
	</p>

	<task label="chkpt-derivative-transfer-1"><title> Derivative Transfer Technique </title>
		<statement>
			<p>
				Which concept from calculus allows you to transfer the derivative of one function to another inside an integral?
			</p>
		</statement>
		<choices randomize="yes">
			<choice>
				<statement>Chain Rule</statement>
				<feedback>
					<p>
						Incorrect. The chain rule is used for function compositions, not transferring derivatives within an integral.
					</p>
				</feedback>
			</choice>
			<choice>
				<statement>L'H√¥pital's Rule</statement>
				<feedback>
					<p>
						Incorrect. L'H√¥pital's Rule helps evaluate indeterminate limits, not manipulate derivatives inside integrals.
					</p>
				</feedback>
			</choice>
			<choice correct="yes">
				<statement>Integration by Parts</statement>
				<feedback>
					<p>
						Correct! Integration by parts is the key tool that shifts derivatives between functions inside integrals.
					</p>
				</feedback>
			</choice>
			<choice>
				<statement>Implicit Differentiation</statement>
				<feedback>
					<p>
						Incorrect. Implicit differentiation helps with equations where the dependent variable isn't isolated.
					</p>
				</feedback>
			</choice>
		</choices>
	</task>

	<p>
		Let's try this with a specific example. Suppose <m>u(t) = e^{-st}</m> and <m>v(t) = y(t)</m>. Then <m>u'(t) = -se^{-st}</m> and <m>v'(t) = y'(t)</m>, so the formula becomes:
		<me>
		\int_{a}^{b} \os{{\color{RoyalBlue}\large\text{derivative on }y}}{ 
			e^{-st} \ {\color{RoyalBlue}\os{\downarrow}{y'\vphantom{\Large\Gamma}}(t)}\ dt
		} = e^{-st} \ y(t) \Big|_{a}^{b}
		- \int_{a}^{b} \os{{\color{RoyalBlue}\large\text{derivative on }e^{-st}}}{ 
			{\color{RoyalBlue}\os{\downarrow}{\left(-s e^{-st}\right)\vphantom{\Large\Gamma}}}\ y(t)\ dt
		}
		</me>
	</p>

	<aside><title> Clarification </title>
		<p>
		Since the integral is in terms of <m>t</m>, <m>s</m> is a constant and can be pulled out of the integral.
		</p>
	</aside>

	<task label="chkpt-derivative-transfer-2"><title> Key Concepts Behind Laplace Transforms </title>
		<statement>
			<p>
				Which two core mathematical ideas does the Laplace transform method rely on to solve differential equations?
			</p>
		</statement>
		<choices randomize="yes">
			<choice>
				<statement>Chain Rule</statement>
				<feedback>
					<p>
						Incorrect. The chain rule is not central to the Laplace transform method. Think about a process that lets you transfer derivatives.
					</p>
				</feedback>
			</choice>
			<choice>
				<statement>Product Rule</statement>
				<feedback>
					<p>
						No, the product rule is not a key idea in the Laplace transform method. Consider an integration-based technique.
					</p>
				</feedback>
			</choice>
			<choice correct="yes">
				<statement>Integration by Parts</statement>
				<feedback>
					<p>
						Correct! Integration by parts allows derivatives to be transferred between functions inside an integral, a foundational technique in the Laplace method.
					</p>
				</feedback>
			</choice>
			<choice correct="yes">
				<statement>Properties of Exponential Functions</statement>
				<feedback>
					<p>
						Correct! The method uses properties of exponential functions, especially their predictable behavior under differentiation and integration.
					</p>
				</feedback>
			</choice>
		</choices>
	</task>
	
	<p>
		The derivative that was initially on <m>y'</m> is now on <m>e^{-st}</m>. Simplifying the integral on the right-hand side yields:
		<me>
		\int_{a}^{b} e^{-st} y'(t) dt = \ub{e^{-st} \ y(t) \Big|_{a}^{b}  + s \int_{a}^{b} e^{-st} \, y(t) \, dt}_{\large\text{no derivatives of }y}
		</me>
	</p>

	<p>
		We can now make two important observations. First, the left-hand side, which originally had a derivative on <m>y</m>, has been rewritten with no derivatives of <m>y</m> at all. Second, the only difference between the two integrals is a factor of <m>s</m>. In a way, the derivative has been encoded inside <m>s</m>.
	</p>

	<task label="chkpt-derivative-transfer-4"><title> Integration by Parts on <m>t^2 y'</m> </title>
		<statement>
			<p>
				Select the result of applying integration by parts to 
				<me>
					\int t^2 \ y'\ dt
				</me>.
			</p>
			<p>
				<em>Don't think of this as a Calculus exercise. Instead, try to think about transferring derivatives.</em>
			</p>
		</statement>
		<choices randomize="yes">
			<choice>
				<statement><m>\ds \int t^2 \ y'\ dt = t^2 y - \int y\ dt</m></statement>
				<feedback>
					<p>
						Incorrect. A correct application of integration by parts must include the right choice of <m>u</m> and <m>dv</m>. Try again.
					</p>
				</feedback>
			</choice>
			<choice>
				<statement><m>\ds \int t^2 \ y'\ dt = t^2 y - \int t^2 y\ dt</m></statement>
				<feedback>
					<p>
						Incorrect. Be sure you're transferring the derivative and integrating the right part.
					</p>
				</feedback>
			</choice>
			<choice>
				<statement><m>\ds \int t^2 \ y'\ dt = 2\int t y\ dt</m></statement>
				<feedback>
					<p>
						Incorrect. This result skips important parts of the integration by parts formula.
					</p>
				</feedback>
			</choice>
			<choice correct="yes">
				<statement><m>\ds \int t^2 \ y'\ dt = t^2 y - 2\int t y\ dt</m></statement>
				<feedback>
					<p>
						Correct! Integration by parts with <m>u = t^2</m> and <m>dv = y'\,dt</m> gives this result.
					</p>
				</feedback>
			</choice>
		</choices>
	</task>

	<p>
		This idea, transferring derivatives onto <m>e^{-st}</m> and ending up with a multiple of the original function, is exactly what makes the Laplace transform method work. We'll explore that in the next section.
	</p>

	<task label="chkpt-derivative-transfer-summary"><title> Reflection </title>
		<statement>
			<p>
				In your own words, explain how integration by parts allows the Laplace transform method to ‚Äúremove‚Äù derivatives from a differential equation. What role does the exponential <m>e^{-st}</m> play?
			</p>
		</statement>
		<response />
	</task>
</subsection>

<subsection><title> Laplace Transform Definition </title>
	<lemma><title> üëÄ <em>Reading Preparation</em> </title>

		<p>
			<task label="prep-lt-def-1"><title> Exponential Integration Warm-Up </title>
				<statement>
					<p>
						Evaluate: <m>\ds\int_0^b e^{-3t}\ dt</m>
					</p>
				</statement>
				<choices randomize="yes">
					<choice correct="yes"><statement><m>\quad\ds \frac{1 - e^{-3b}}{3}</m></statement></choice>
					<choice><statement><m>\quad\ds \frac{1}{3}e^{-3b}</m></statement></choice>
					<choice><statement><m>\quad\ds -\frac{1}{3}e^{-3b}</m></statement></choice>
					<choice><statement><m>\quad\ds -\frac{1}{3}e^{3b}</m></statement></choice>
				</choices>
			</task>
	
			<task label="prep-lt-def-2"><title> Exponential Behavior </title>
				<statement>
					<p>
						What happens to <m>e^{-st}</m> as <m>t \to \infty</m> when <m>s > 0</m>?
					</p>
				</statement>
				<choices randomize="yes">
					<choice correct="yes"><statement>It decays to 0.</statement></choice>
					<choice><statement>It grows to infinity.</statement></choice>
					<choice><statement>It stays constant.</statement></choice>
					<choice><statement>It oscillates.</statement></choice>
				</choices>
			</task>
		</p>
			
	</lemma>

	<p>
		In the previous section, we saw that multiplying a differential equation by <m>e^{-st}</m> and integrating allows us to shift derivatives away from the unknown function and onto the exponential. However, most differential equations don't come with an <m>e^{-st}</m> built in, we have to introduce it ourselves.
	</p>

	<p>
		Let's explore this process using a concrete example.
	</p>

	<p>
		Suppose we are given the equation:
		<men xml:id="lt-model-eqn">
		y'' - 9y = 10e^{2t}
		</men>
	</p>

	<p>
		We multiply both sides by <m>e^{-st}</m> and integrate with respect to <m>t</m>:
		<me>
		\int \left(y'' - 9y\right) e^{-st} \, dt = \int 10e^{2t} e^{-st} \, dt.
		</me>
		Using the linearity of the integral, we can rewrite this as:
		<me>
		\ub{\int e^{-st} y''\ dt}_{\text{transfer 2 derivatives}}
		- 9\ub{\int e^{-st} y\ dt}_{\text{leave as-is}}
		= \ub{\int e^{-st} \left( 10e^{2t} \right)\ dt}_{\text{integrate directly}}.
		</me>
	</p>

	<p>
		This transformation rewrites the differential equation into a form where each term is ready for conversion, either by transferring derivatives or by direct integration. This leads us to the central idea behind the Laplace transform.
	</p>

	<task label="chkpt-lt-defn-1"><title> Key Steps in Finding a Laplace Transform </title>
		<statement>
		<p>
			Fill-in-the-blank: The process of finding a Laplace transform involves multiplying the original function by <m>\ul{\hspace{5em}}</m> and <m>\ul{\hspace{5em}}</m> with respect to <m>t</m>.
		</p>
		</statement>
		<choices randomize="yes">
		<choice>
			<statement><m>e^{-t},</m> integrating</statement>
			<feedback>
			<p>
				No, this misses the <m>s</m> parameter in the exponential. It should be <m>e^{-st}</m>.
			</p>
			</feedback>
		</choice>
		<choice correct="yes">
			<statement><m>e^{-st},</m> integrating</statement>
			<feedback>
			<p>
				Correct! The Laplace transform involves multiplying the original function by <m>e^{-st}</m> and then integrating with respect to <m>t</m>.
			</p>
			</feedback>
		</choice>
		<choice>
			<statement><m>e^{st},</m> integrating</statement>
			<feedback>
			<p>
				No, the exponent should have a negative sign: <m>e^{-st}</m>.
			</p>
			</feedback>
		</choice>
		<choice>
			<statement><m>e^{-st},</m> differentiating</statement>
			<feedback>
			<p>
				No, the Laplace transform requires integration, not differentiation.
			</p>
			</feedback>
		</choice>
		<choice>
			<statement><m>s,</m> differentiating</statement>
			<feedback>
			<p>
				No, the transform uses <m>e^{-st}</m> and integration, not the variable <m>s</m> directly or differentiation.
			</p>
			</feedback>
		</choice>
		</choices>
	</task>

	<definition xml:id="laplace-transform-definition"><title>Laplace Transform</title>
		<statement>
			<p>
				Given a function <m>f(t)</m> defined for <m>t \geq 0</m>, its Laplace transform, denoted <m>\lap{f(t)}</m>, is defined by
				<me>
					\lap{f(t)} = \int_0^{\infty} e^{-st} f(t) \, dt,
				</me>
				provided the integral converges. The result is a new function, typically written as <m>F(s)</m>:
				<me>
					F(s) = \lap{f(t)}.
				</me>
			</p>
		</statement>
	</definition>

	<task label="chkpt-lt-defn-2"><title> Identify the Error in the Laplace Transform Definition </title>
		<statement>
		<p>
			Click on the incorrect part of the Laplace transform definition.
		</p>
		</statement>
		<areas>
		<p/>
		<sidebyside widths="28% 44% 28%">
			<p/>
			<p>
			<area correct="no"><m>\ds \laplacesym</m></area>
			<m>\big\{</m>
			<area correct="no"><m>f(t)</m></area>
			<m>\big\}</m>
			<m>=</m>
			<area correct="no"><m>\ds \int_{0}^{\infty}</m></area>
			<area correct="yes"><m>\ds e^{st}</m></area>
			<area correct="no"><m>\ds f(t)</m></area>
			<area correct="no"><m>\ds dt</m></area>
			</p>
			<p/>
		</sidebyside>
		</areas>
	</task>

	<p>
		Since the upper limit of the integral is <m>\infty</m>, this is an improper integral. From calculus, you might recall that improper integrals are evaluated by taking a limit:
		<me>
			\int_0^{\infty} e^{-st} f(t)\, dt = \ub{\lim_{b \to \infty} \ob{\int_0^b e^{-st} f(t)\, dt}^{\large\text{(step 1)}}}_{\large\text{(step 2)}}
		</me>
	</p>

	<p>
		During the first step, treat <m>s</m> and <m>b</m> as constants and compute the integral with respect to <m>t</m>. Then, in the second step, take the limit as <m>b \to \infty</m>. If the result converges to a finite value, the Laplace transform exists.
	</p>

	<task label="chkpt-lt-defn-3"><title> Convergence Requirement for Laplace Transforms </title>
		<statement correct="yes">
			<p>
				The Laplace transform only exists if the improper integral,
				<me>
					\lap{ f(t) } = \int_0^{\infty} e^{-st} f(t)\, dt
				</me>
				converges.
			</p>
		</statement>
		<feedback>
			<p>
				This statement is true. The Laplace transform is defined by an improper integral, and it only exists when this integral converges. If the integral diverges, the Laplace transform does not exist.
			</p>
		</feedback>
	</task>

	<p>
		Let's walk through two examples to see how this definition works in practice.
	</p>

	<example xml:id="laplace-example-constant"><title> Laplace Transform of a Constant Function </title>
		<p>
			<statement>
				<p>
					Find the Laplace transform of the constant function <m>f(t) = 5</m>.
				</p>
			</statement>
		</p>

		<solution><title> <m>\rightarrow \lap{5}</m> </title>
			<p>
				By the definition above, we have the improper integral
				<men xml:id="lt-def-example-eqn-1">
					\lap{5} = \int_0^{\infty} e^{-st} \cdot 5\ dt = \lim_{b \to \infty} \ub{\int_0^b 5e^{-st}dt}_{I}
				</men>.
			</p>

			<p>
				Assuming <m>s</m> and <m>b</m> are constant, we integrate <m>I</m> with respect to <m>t</m>:
				<me>
					I = \int_0^b 5e^{-st}dt = -\frac{5}{s}e^{-st}\Big|_{t=0}^{t=b} = -\frac{5}{s}\left[ e^{-sb} - 1 \right]
				</me>.
			</p>

			<p>
				Next, we compute the limit of <m>I</m> as <m>b \to \infty</m> (with <m>s</m> constant):
				<md>
					<mrow>
						\lim_{b \to \infty} I = \lim_{b \to \infty} -\frac{5}{s}\left[ e^{-sb} - 1 \right] 
							\amp <xref text="custom" ref="lt-example-1-details"><m>\os{\large ‚ùî}{=}</m></xref> -\frac{5}{s} \lim_{b \to \infty} \Big[ e^{-sb} - 1 \Big]
					</mrow>
					<mrow>
							\amp = -\frac{5}{s} \Big[ \ub{\lim_{b \to \infty} e^{-sb}}_{L} - 1 \Big]
					</mrow>
				</md>
			</p>
	
			<p>
				Since the limit, <m>L</m> as <m>b \to \infty</m>, depends on the <term><xref ref="graph-of-e-to-x" text="custom">power of the exponential function</xref></term>, there are two cases based on the sign of <m>s</m>:
				<aside>
					<p>
						Note, <m>s = 0</m> is not possible since <m>I</m> contains <m>\sfrac{5}{s}</m> and would be undefined.
					</p>
				</aside>
				<ol>
					<li> <m>s &gt; 0</m>: <m>\ -sb \to -\infty\ </m>, so <m>\ e^{-sb} \to 0\ </m> &amp; <m>L=0\ </m> (converges) </li>
					<li> <m>s &lt; 0</m>: <m>\ -sb \to +\infty\ </m>, so <m>\ e^{-sb} \to \infty\ </m> &amp; <m>L=\infty\ </m> (diverges) </li>
				</ol>
				By definition, the integral in a Laplace transform must converge, meaning <m>L</m> must converge. So, <m>L=0</m> and the Laplace transform is
				<me>
					\lap{5} = -\frac{1}{s} [0 - 1] = \frac{1}{s} \quad \text{for } s \gt 0
				</me>.
			</p>
		</solution>
	</example>

	<example><title> Laplace Transform of <m>t</m> </title>
		<p>
			<statement>
				<p>
					Find the Laplace transform of the function <m>f(t)=t</m>.
				</p>
			</statement>
		</p>
		<solution xml:id="laplace-transform-of-t-power-1"><title> <m>\rightarrow \lap{t}</m> </title>

			<p>
				Applying the definition and writing the integral as a limit, we have
				<me>
					\lap{ t }	= \lim_{b \to \infty} {\color{BurntOrange}\ub{\int_0^b e^{-st} \cdot t\ dt}_{I}}
				</me>.
			</p>

			<p>
				Computing <xref text="custom" ref="lt-example-t-IVP-details"><m>I</m> with integration by parts</xref>, gives us
				<md>
					<mrow>
						{\color{BurntOrange}I}	\amp\ {\color{BurntOrange} = -\frac{b}{s}e^{-sb} - \frac{1}{s^2}e^{-sb} + \frac{1}{s^2}}
					</mrow>
				</md>
			</p>

			<p>
				Next, we compute the limit of <m>I</m> as <m>b \to \infty</m>:
				<md>
					<mrow>
						\lim_{b \to \infty} {\color{BurntOrange}I} \amp = \lim_{b \to \infty} \left({\color{BurntOrange} -\frac{b}{s}e^{-sb} - \frac{1}{s^2}e^{-sb} + \frac{1}{s^2}}\right)
					</mrow>
					<mrow>
						\amp = -\frac{1}{s}\lim_{b \to \infty} be^{-sb} - \frac{1}{s^2}\lim_{b \to \infty} e^{-sb} + \lim_{b \to \infty} \frac{1}{s^2}
					</mrow>
					<mrow>
						\amp = -\frac{1}{s}\ub{\lim_{b \to \infty} \frac{b}{e^{sb}}}_{L_1} - \frac{1}{s^2}\ub{\lim_{b \to \infty} e^{-sb}}_{L_2} + \frac{1}{s^2}
					</mrow>
				</md> 
			</p>

			<p>
				As long as <m>s \gt 0</m>, both <term><xref ref="lt-example-t-LH-details" text="custom"><m>L_1\to 0</m> &amp; <m>L_2\to 0</m></xref></term>. Therefore, the Laplace transform of <m>t</m> is
				<me>
					\lap{ t } = \frac{1}{s^2}, \quad s &gt; 0
				</me>.
			</p>
		</solution>
	</example>

	<p>
		These examples illustrate the typical process of computing a Laplace transform. Moving forward, we'll use this definition to build a table of transforms, which will become a powerful tool for solving differential equations.
	</p>
</subsection>

<subsection><title> Common Transforms </title>
	<lemma><title> üëÄ <em>Reading Preparation</em> </title>

		<p>
			<task label="prep-common-transforms-1"><title> Exponential Integration Rules </title>
				<statement>
					<p>
						Match each exponential integrand with its antiderivative. Assume <m>K</m> is a constant.
					</p>
				</statement>
				<cardsort>
					<match>
						<premise><m>\ds \int e^{5t} dt</m></premise>
						<response><m>\ds \frac{1}{5} e^{5t} + C</m></response>
					</match>
					<match>
						<premise><m>\ds \int e^{-3t} dt</m></premise>
						<response><m>\ds \frac{-1}{3} e^{-3t} + C</m></response>
					</match>
					<match>
						<premise><m>\ds \int e^{Kt} dt</m></premise>
						<response><m>\ds \frac{1}{K} e^{Kt} + C</m></response>
					</match>
					<match>
						<premise><m>\ds \int e^{(7-s)t} dt</m></premise>
						<response><m>\ds \frac{1}{7 - s} e^{(7-s)t} + C</m></response>
					</match>
				</cardsort>
			</task>
	
			<task label="prep-common-transforms-2"><title> How is <m>s</m> Treated During Integration? </title>
				<statement>
					<p>
						In the Laplace transform integral, the variable <m>s</m> is treated as a <fillin characters="10" /> during the integration process.
					</p>
				</statement>
				<choices randomize="yes">
					<choice correct="yes">
						<statement>constant</statement>
						<feedback>
							<p>
								Correct! When evaluating the integral, <m>s</m> is treated as a constant, since the integration is with respect to <m>t</m>.
							</p>
						</feedback>
					</choice>
					<choice>
						<statement>variable</statement>
						<feedback>
							<p>
								No, even though <m>s</m> is a variable overall, it is treated as a constant during the integration.
							</p>
						</feedback>
					</choice>
					<choice>
						<statement>coefficient</statement>
						<feedback>
							<p>
								No, while <m>s</m> acts like a coefficient in <m>e^{-st}</m>, it's conceptually treated as a constant in the integration.
							</p>
						</feedback>
					</choice>
					<choice>
						<statement>limit</statement>
						<feedback>
							<p>
								No, <m>s</m> is not a limit of integration, it appears inside the integrand.
							</p>
						</feedback>
					</choice>
				</choices>
			</task>
	
			<task label="prep-common-transforms-3"><title> Choosing <m>u</m> and <m>dv</m> in <m>\int t^2 e^{-st} dt</m> </title>
				<statement>
					<p>
						Which functions should you choose as <m>u</m> and <m>dv</m> when applying integration by parts to <m>\int t^2 e^{-st} dt</m>?
					</p>
				</statement>
				<choices randomize="yes">
					<choice correct="yes">
						<statement><m>u = t^2,\quad dv = e^{-st} dt</m></statement>
						<feedback>
							<p>Correct! This choice simplifies with each integration by parts.</p>
						</feedback>
					</choice>
					<choice>
						<statement><m>u = e^{-st},\quad dv = t^2 dt</m></statement>
						<feedback>
							<p>No, this makes integration more difficult. We want to differentiate <m>t^2</m> and integrate the exponential.</p>
						</feedback>
					</choice>
					<choice>
						<statement><m>u = st,\quad dv = t dt</m></statement>
						<feedback>
							<p>These choices are not aligned with the integrand <m>t^2 e^{-st}</m>.</p>
						</feedback>
					</choice>
				</choices>
			</task>
	
		</p>
	
	</lemma>

	<p>
		Now that we've defined the Laplace transform, it's time to build our toolbox. In this section, we'll compute the Laplace transforms of several commonly used functions:
		<me>
			1, \quad e^{at}, \quad t^n, \quad \sin(bt), \quad \text{and} \quad \cos(bt)
		</me>.
		Since these functions appear so frequently in differential equations, having a general formula for their transforms will be vital in the solution process.
	</p>

	<p>
		Since the process for computing the Laplace transform of the constant function <m>f(t) = 1</m> mirrors what we did for <m>f(t) = 5</m> in the previous section, we'll simply state the result here:
		<me>
			\lap{1} = \frac{1}{s}, \quad s \gt 0.
		</me>
	</p>

	<p>
		The transforms of other basic functions are computed in the examples that follow, beginning with exponential functions.
	</p>

	<example><title> Laplace Transform of an Exponential Function </title>
		<p>
			<statement>
				<p>
					Compute <m>\lap{e^{7t}}</m>, then use it as a template to determine the formula
					<me>
						\lap{e^{at}} = \frac{1}{s - a}, \quad s \gt a
					</me>.
				</p>
			</statement>
		</p>

		<p>
			<solution><title> <m>\rightarrow \lap{e^{7t}}</m> </title>
				<p>
					Applying the definition of the Laplace transform and writing the integral as a limit, we have
					<me>
						\lap{ e^{7t} }
						= \int_0^{\infty} e^{-st} \cdot e^{7t}\ dt
						= \lim_{b \to \infty}\int_0^b e^{(7-s)t}\ dt
					</me>.
				</p>

				<aside>
					<p>
						For any constant <m>K</m>, 
						<me>
							\int e^{K x} dx = \frac{1}{K} e^{K x} + C
						</me>
						by u-substitution.
					</p>
				</aside>

				<p>
					Focusing on the integral first and treating <m>7-s</m> as constant, we have
					<md>
						<mrow>
							\int_0^b e^{(7-s)t}\, dt
							<xref text="custom" ref="lt-example-2-details"><m>\os{\large ‚ùî}{=}</m></xref> 
							\frac{1}{7-s} e^{(7-s)t} \Bigg|_0^b
							\amp = \frac{1}{7-s} \left( e^{(7-s)b} - e^{0} \right)
						</mrow>
						<mrow>
							\amp = \frac{1}{7-s} \left( e^{(7-s)b} - 1 \right)
						</mrow>
					</md>
				</p>

				<p>
					Now, we compute the limit of this expression as <m>b \to \infty</m>:
					<md>
						<mrow>
							\lim_{b \to \infty} \frac{1}{7-s} \left( e^{(7-s)b} - 1 \right)
							\amp = \frac{1}{7-s} \Bigg[ \ub{\lim_{b \to \infty} e^{(7-s)b}}_{L} - \lim_{b \to \infty} 1 \Bigg]
						</mrow>
					</md>.
				</p>

				<p>
					As before, the <term><xref ref="graph-of-e-to-x" text="custom">limit of the exponential function</xref></term> converges if its power, <m>(7-s)b</m>, goes to <m>-\infty</m>. This happens if <m>7-s</m> is negative (i.e., <m>s \gt 7</m>). 
				</p>

				<p>
					So, as long as <m>s \gt 7</m>, then <m>L \to 0</m> and the transform becomes 
					<me>
						\lap{e^{7t}} = \frac{1}{s - 7}, \quad s \gt 7
					</me>.
				</p>
			</solution>

			<solution><title> <m>\rightarrow \lap{e^{at}}</m> </title>
				<p>
					By swapping every <m>7</m> with an <m>a</m> in the previous solution, you will arrive at the following formula for the common Laplace transform:
					<me>
						\lap{e^{at}} = \frac{1}{s - a}, \quad s \gt a
					</me>,
					where <m>a</m> is a constant.
				</p>
			</solution>
		</p>
	</example>

	<task label="chkpt-common-exp-1"><title> Convergence in <m>\lap{e^{7t}}</m> </title>
		<statement>
			<p>
				Why does the integral for <m>\lap{e^{7t}}</m> converge only if <m>s \gt 7</m>?
			</p>
		</statement>
		<choices randomize="yes">
			<choice>
				<statement>The integral becomes zero when <m>s = 7</m></statement>
				<feedback>
					<p>No, <m>s = 7</m> makes the exponent zero, which leads to divergence.</p>
				</feedback>
			</choice>
			<choice correct="yes">
				<statement>The exponential term goes to zero only if <m>7 - s \lt 0</m></statement>
				<feedback>
					<p>Correct! This guarantees the exponent goes to <m>-\infty</m> as <m>t \to \infty</m>.</p>
				</feedback>
			</choice>
			<choice>
				<statement>Laplace transforms require <m>s</m> to be greater than any exponent</statement>
				<feedback>
					<p>That's a decent rule of thumb, but we need to explain it in terms of convergence of the integral.</p>
				</feedback>
			</choice>
			<choice>
				<statement>The integral does not depend on <m>s</m></statement>
				<feedback>
					<p>No, the convergence completely depends on the sign of <m>7 - s</m>.</p>
				</feedback>
			</choice>
		</choices>
	</task>		

	<p>
		Next, we compute the transforms of the polynomial terms, <m>t</m> and <m>t^2</m>, and use the result to uncover a recursive structure that helps generalize to <m>t^n</m>.
	</p>

	<example><title> Laplace Transform of the Power Function </title>
		<p>
			<statement>
				<p>
					Show that you can relate the Laplace transforms of <m>t</m> and <m>t^2</m> by
					<me>
						\lap{t^2} = \frac{2}{s}\cdot \lap{t}, \quad s \gt 0
					</me>.
					Using this as a template, derive the general recursive relationship
					<me>
						\lap{t^n} = \frac{n}{s}\cdot \lap{t^{n-1}}, \quad s \gt 0
					</me>.
					Finally, hypothesize the general formula for the Laplace transform of <m>t^n</m>,
					<me>
						\lap{t^n} = \frac{n!}{s^{n+1}}, \quad s \gt 0
					</me>.
				</p>
			</statement>
		</p>
		<p>
			<solution><title> <m>\rightarrow \lap{t^2}</m> </title>
				<p>
					Applying the definition and writing the integral as a limit, we have
					<me>
						\lap{ t^2 } = \lim_{b \to \infty} \ub{\int_0^b e^{-st} \cdot t^2\ dt}_{I}
					</me>.
				</p>

				<p>
					Applying integration by parts to <m>I</m> once in <m>\lap{ t^2 }</m> shows
					<md>
						<mrow>
							\lap{ t^2 }
							\amp = \lim_{b \to \infty} \left[-\frac{b^2}{s}e^{-sb} + \frac{2}{s}\int_0^b e^{-st}\cdot t\ dt\right]
							<xref ref="lt-example-tsqrd-IVP-details" text="custom">üîçHow?</xref>
						</mrow>
						<mrow>
							\amp = -\frac{1}{s} \lim_{b \to \infty} \left[-\frac{b^2}{e^{sb}}\right] + \frac{2}{s}\lim_{b \to \infty}\int_0^b e^{-st}\cdot t\ dt
						</mrow>
						<mrow>
							\amp = -\frac{1}{s}\ub{\lim_{b \to \infty}\frac{b^2}{e^{sb}}}_{L} + \frac{2}{s}\ub{\int_0^\infty e^{-st}\cdot t\ dt}_{\large\lap{t}}
						</mrow>.
					</md>
				</p>

				<p>
					Noticing that the improper integral is just <m>\lap{t}</m>, we have
					<me>
						\lap{ t^2 } = -\frac{1}{s}\cdot L + \frac{2}{s}\cdot \lap{t}
					</me>,
					but <m>L=0</m> if <m>s \gt 0</m> <xref ref="lt-example-tsqrd-LH-details" text="custom">(üîçHow?)</xref>, so we have the relationship
				</p>

				<me>
					\lap{t^2} = \frac{2}{s}\cdot \lap{t}, \quad s \gt 0
				</me>.
			</solution>

			<solution><title> <m>\rightarrow \lap{t^n}</m> (recursive) </title>
				<p>
					Replacing <m>2</m> with <m>n</m> in the previous solution, we have
					<me>
						\lap{ t^n } = \lim_{b \to \infty} \ub{\int_0^b e^{-st} \cdot t^n\ dt}_{I}
					</me>.
				</p>

				<p>
					Applying integration by parts to <m>I</m> once in <m>\lap{ t^{n} }</m> shows
					<md>
						<mrow>
							\lap{ t^{n} }
							\amp = \lim_{b \to \infty} \left[-\frac{b^n}{e^{sb}} + \frac{n}{s}\int_0^b e^{-st}\cdot t^{n-1}\ dt\right]
							<xref ref="lt-example-tnth-IVP-details" text="custom">üîçHow?</xref>
						</mrow>
						<mrow>
							\amp = -\frac{1}{s} \lim_{b \to \infty} \left[-\frac{b^n}{e^{sb}}\right] + \frac{n}{s}\lim_{b \to \infty}\int_0^b e^{-st}\cdot t^{n-1}\ dt
						</mrow>
						<mrow>
							\amp = -\frac{1}{s}\ub{\lim_{b \to \infty}\frac{b^n}{e^{sb}}}_{L} + \frac{n}{s}\ub{\int_0^\infty e^{-st}\cdot t^{n-1}\ dt}_{\large\lap{t^{n-1}}}
						</mrow>.
					</md>
				</p>

				<p>
					Noticing that the improper integral is just <m>\lap{t^{n-1}}</m>, we have
					<me>
						\lap{ t^{n} } = -\frac{1}{s}\cdot L + \frac{n}{s}\cdot \lap{t^{n-1}}
					</me>,
					but <m>L=0</m> if <m>s \gt 0</m> <xref ref="lt-example-tnth-LH-details" text="custom">(üîçHow?)</xref>, so we have the relationship
				</p>

				<me>
					\lap{t^{n}} = \frac{n}{s}\cdot \lap{t^{n-1}}, \quad s \gt 0
				</me>.
			</solution>

			<solution><title> <m>\rightarrow \lap{t^n}</m> (formula) </title>
				<p>
					Starting with the recursive relationship for <m>t^n</m>, we can apply it repeatedly to find the Laplace transform of <m>t^n</m> in terms of <m>t^0 = 1</m>. This gives us
					<md>
						<mrow>
							\lap{t^n} 
							\amp = \frac{n}{s}\cdot \ub{\lap{t^{n-1}}}_{\downarrow}
						</mrow>
						<mrow>
							\amp = \frac{n}{s}\cdot \Big(\frac{n-1}{s}\cdot \ub{\lap{t^{n-2}}}_{\downarrow}\Big)
						</mrow>
						<mrow>
							\amp = \frac{n(n-1)}{s^2}\cdot \Big(\frac{n-2}{s}\cdot \ub{\lap{t^{n-3}}}_{\downarrow}\Big)
						</mrow>
						<mrow>
							\amp \qquad\qquad\vdots
						</mrow>
						<mrow>
							\amp = \frac{n(n-1)\ldots 4}{s^{n-3}}\cdot \Big(\frac{3}{s}\cdot \lap{t^{2}}\Big)
						</mrow>
						<mrow>
							\amp = \frac{n(n-1)\ldots 4\cdot 3}{s^{n-2}}\cdot \Big(\frac{2}{s^3}\Big)
						</mrow>
						<mrow>
							\amp = \frac{n!}{s^{n+1}}
						</mrow>
					</md>.
				</p>
				<p>
					Thus, the Laplace transform of <m>t^n</m> is 
					<me>
						\lap{t^n} = \frac{n!}{s^{n+1}}, \quad s \gt 0, \quad n = 0, 1, 2, \ldots
					</me>.
				</p>

			</solution>
		</p>
	</example>

	<task label="chkpt-common-poly-2"><title> Limit of <m>b^3/e^{6b}</m> </title>
		<statement>
			<p>
				What is the value of <m>\ds \lim_{b \to \infty} \frac{b^3}{e^{6b}}</m>?
			</p>
		</statement>
		<choices randomize="yes">
			<choice>
				<statement><m>\infty</m></statement>
				<feedback>
					<p>No, exponential growth (<m>e^{6b}</m>) grows faster than polynomial growth (<m>b^3</m>) as <m>b \to \infty</m>. This pulls the ratio, <m>\frac{b^3}{e^{6b}}</m>, to zero.</p>
				</feedback>
			</choice>
			<choice correct="yes">
				<statement><m>0</m></statement>
				<feedback>
					<p>Correct, exponential growth (<m>e^{6b}</m>) grows faster than polynomial growth (<m>b^3</m>) as <m>b \to \infty</m>. This pulls the ratio, <m>\frac{b^3}{e^{6b}}</m>, to zero. This can also be verified with L'H√¥pital's rule.</p>
				</feedback>
			</choice>
			<choice>
				<statement><m>b^3</m></statement>
				<feedback>
					<p>No, <m>b</m> goes to <m>\infty</m>. So the limit cannot have <m>b</m> in its value.</p>
				</feedback>
			</choice>
			<choice>
				<statement><m>6</m></statement>
				<feedback>
					<p>No, exponential growth (<m>e^{6b}</m>) grows faster than polynomial growth (<m>b^3</m>) as <m>b \to \infty</m>. This pulls the ratio, <m>\frac{b^3}{e^{6b}}</m>, to zero.</p>
				</feedback>
			</choice>
		</choices>
	</task>
	
	<p>
		Finally, we move on to trigonometric functions, which show up in systems with oscillatory behavior. The transform of <m>\cos(bt)</m> is shown here, which provides a model for deriving the corresponding sine transform.
	</p>

	<example><title> Laplace Transform of Cosine </title>
		<p>
			<statement>
				<p>
					Compute <m>\lap{\cos(3t)}</m>, then use it as a template to determine the formula
					<me>
						\lap{\cos(bt)} = \frac{s}{s^2 + b^2}, \quad s \gt 0
					</me>.
				</p>
			</statement>
		</p>
		
		<p>
			<solution><title> <m>\rightarrow \lap{\cos(3t)}</m> </title>
				<p>
					Applying the definition and writing the integral as a limit, we have
					<me>
						\lap{ \cos(3t)} = \int_0^{\infty} e^{-st} \cdot \cos(3t)\ dt = \lim_{b \to \infty} \ub{\int_0^{b} e^{-st} \cos(3t)\ dt}_{I}
					</me>.
				</p>

				<p>
					Treating <m>s</m> constant, we apply integration by parts to <m>I</m> as follows:
				</p>

				<me>
					I = -\frac{e^{-st}}{s} \cos(3t) \Bigg|_0^b - \frac{3}{s}\int_0^{b} e^{-st} \sin(3t)\ dt
				</me>,

				<aside><title> Integration by Parts </title>
					<p>
						1st Integration by Parts
						<md>
							<mrow>	u	\amp = \cos(3t)				</mrow>
							<mrow>	du	\amp = -3\sin(3t)\ dt		</mrow>
							<mrow>	v	\amp = -\sfrac{e^{-st}}{s}	</mrow>
							<mrow>	dv	\amp = e^{-st}\ dt			</mrow>
						</md>
						2nd Integration by Parts
						<md>
							<mrow>	u	\amp = \sin(3t)				</mrow>
							<mrow>	du	\amp = 3\cos(3t)\ dt		</mrow>
							<mrow>	v	\amp = -\sfrac{e^{-st}}{s}	</mrow>
							<mrow>	dv	\amp = e^{-st}\ dt			</mrow>
						</md>
					</p>
				</aside>

				<p>
					but this requires a second integration by parts:
					<md>
						<mrow>
							I
							\amp = -\frac{e^{-st}}{s} \cos(3t) \Bigg|_0^b - \frac{3}{s} \left[ -\frac{e^{-st}}{s} \sin(3t)\Bigg|_0^b + \frac{3}{s} \int_0^{b} e^{-st} \cos(3t)\ dt \right]
						</mrow>
						<mrow>
							\amp = \left(-\frac{e^{-st}}{s} \cos(3t) + \frac{3e^{-st}}{s^2} \sin(3t)\right)\Bigg|_0^b - \frac{9}{s^2} \ub{\int_0^{b} e^{-st} \cos(3t)\ dt}_{I}
						</mrow>
					</md>.
				</p>

				<p>
					Since the integral <m>I</m> reappeared, we actually have
					<me>
						I = \left(-\frac{e^{-st}}{s} \cos(3t) + \frac{3e^{-st}}{s^2} \sin(3t)\right)\Bigg|_0^b - \frac{9}{s^2} I
					</me>.
					Evaluating the limits of integration and solving for <m>I</m>, we find
					<md>
						<mrow>
							I + \frac{9}{s^2} I  \amp = \left(-\frac{e^{-st}}{s} \cos(3t) + \frac{3e^{-st}}{s^2} \sin(3t)\right)\Bigg|_0^b
						</mrow>
						<mrow>
							I\left(\frac{s^2+9}{s^2}\right) \amp = \left(-\frac{e^{-sb}}{s} \cos(3b) + \frac{3e^{-sb}}{s^2} \sin(3b) + \frac{1}{s} \right)
						</mrow>
						<mrow> 
							I	\amp = \frac{s^2}{s^2+9}\left(-\frac{e^{-sb}}{s} \cos(3b) + \frac{3e^{-sb}}{s^2} \sin(3b) + \frac{1}{s} \right)
						</mrow>
					</md>
				</p>

				<p>
					Finally, the limit of <m>I</m> as <m>b \to \infty</m> is the sum of the limits
					<md>
						<mrow>
							\lim_{b \to \infty} I = \amp
						</mrow>
						<mrow>
							\frac{s^2}{s^2+9} \amp \Bigg(
								- \frac{1}{s}\ub{\lim_{b \to \infty} e^{-sb} \cos(3b)}_{L_1}
								+ \frac{3}{s^2}\ub{\lim_{b \to \infty} e^{-sb} \sin(3b)}_{L_2} 
								+ \frac{1}{s}
							\Bigg)
						</mrow>
					</md>
					Since <xref ref="lt-example-4-details" text="custom"><m>L_1\to0</m> &amp; <m>L_2\to0</m> if <m>s \gt 0</m></xref>, we can conclude that
					<me>
						\lap{\cos(3t)} = \frac{s}{s^2 + 9}, \quad s \gt 0
					</me>.
				</p>
			</solution>

			<solution><title> <m>\rightarrow \lap{\cos(bt)}</m> </title>
				<p>
					Replacing the constants <m>3</m> and <m>9</m> with <m>b</m> and <m>b^2</m>, respectively, shows that
					<me>
						\lap{\cos(bt)} = \frac{s}{s^2 + b^2}, \quad s \gt 0
					</me>.
				</p>
			</solution>
		</p>
	</example>

	<p>
		The derivation of the formula for the Laplace transform of sine, given by
		<me>
			\lap{\sin(bt)} = \frac{b}{s^2 + b^2}, \quad s \gt 0
		</me>,
		follows a similar approach and is left as exercise for you to try on your own.
	</p>

	<p>
		The transforms derived in this section will serve as the foundational building blocks in the Laplace method. In the next section, we'll expand this toolbox further by exploring the rules and properties of the Laplace transform that allow us to combine and manipulate transforms more flexibly.
	</p>
</subsection>

<subsection><title> Properties and Rules </title>
	<lemma><title> üëÄ <em>Reading Preparation</em> </title>

		<p>
	
			<task label="prep-lt-properties-1"><title> Match the Term Type </title>
				<statement>
					<p>
						Match each expression with its most appropriate mathematical type.
					</p>
				</statement>
				<cardsort>
					<match>
						<premise><m>3f(t) + 2g(t)</m></premise>
						<response>linear combination</response>
					</match>
					<match>
						<premise><m>e^{2t} \cdot f(t)</m></premise>
						<response>exponential shift</response>
					</match>
					<match>
						<premise><m>f'(t)</m></premise>
						<response>derivative</response>
					</match>
					<match>
						<premise><m>t^3 f(t)</m></premise>
						<response>polynomial scaling</response>
					</match>
				</cardsort>
			</task>
	
			<task label="prep-lt-properties-2"><title> Integration Recall </title>
				<statement>
					<p>
						Click on all statements that correctly describe properties of definite integrals.
					</p>
				</statement>
				<areas>
					<p>
						<area correct="yes"><m>\ds \int (af(t) + bg(t))\,dt = a \int f(t)\,dt + b \int g(t)\,dt</m></area>
						<area correct="no"><m>\ds \int f'(t)\,dt = f(t) + C</m></area>
						<area correct="yes"><m>\ds \int t^n e^{-st} dt</m> can be evaluated using integration by parts</area>
						<area correct="no">You cannot take the derivative of an integral</area>
					</p>
				</areas>
			</task>
	
			<task label="prep-lt-properties-3"><title> Spot the Shift </title>
				<statement>
					<p>
						Which of the following expressions represent a shift or modification of a known function? Select all that apply.
					</p>
				</statement>
				<choices multiple="true">
					<choice correct="yes"><statement><m>e^{3t} \cdot \cos(t)</m></statement></choice>
					<choice correct="yes"><statement><m>\sin(2t)</m></statement></choice>
					<choice correct="no"><statement><m>\cos(t) + \sin(t)</m></statement></choice>
					<choice correct="yes"><statement><m>t^2 \cdot f(t)</m></statement></choice>
					<choice correct="no"><statement><m>f(t)^2</m></statement></choice>
				</choices>
			</task>
	
			<task label="prep-lt-properties-4"><title> Derivative Practice </title>
				<statement>
					<p>
						Which of the following are correct derivatives?
					</p>
				</statement>
				<areas>
					<area correct="yes"><m>\frac{d}{dt}[e^{2t}] = 2e^{2t}</m></area>
					<area correct="yes"><m>\frac{d}{dt}[t^3] = 3t^2</m></area>
					<area correct="no"><m>\frac{d}{dt}[\cos(t)] = \cos(t)</m></area>
					<area correct="no"><m>\frac{d}{dt}[5] = 5</m></area>
					<area correct="yes"><m>\frac{d}{dt}[\sin(3t)] = 3\cos(3t)</m></area>
				</areas>
			</task>
			
		</p>
	
	</lemma>

	<p>
		Now that we've built a foundation of basic Laplace transforms, it's time to expand our toolbox by exploring the key properties that make the Laplace transform such a powerful problem-solving technique. These properties allow us to transform combinations of functions, like sums, products, and derivatives, more easily and systematically.
	</p>
	
	<p>
		In this section, you'll learn how the Laplace transform behaves under linear operations, how exponential functions shift the resulting transform, and how to efficiently handle derivatives and polynomial factors. Each property is introduced with a formal statement, illustrated through worked examples, and connected to its role in solving differential equations. Mastery of these rules is essential for applying the Laplace method effectively.
	</p>

	<subsubsection><title> Sums and Constant Multiples (Linearity) </title>
		<p>
			As with integrals, we can pull out constants from Laplace transforms and apply them, term-by-term, to sums and differences of functions. This linear property streamlines the computation of Laplace transforms when working with expressions that combine simpler functions.
		</p>

		<assemblage xml:id="lt-linearity-property"><title> ‚ú≥Ô∏è Laplace Transform Property: Linearity </title>
			<p>
				If <m>a</m> and <m>b</m> are constants, and <m>f(t)</m> and <m>g(t)</m> have Laplace transforms, then
				<me>
					\lap{ a f(t) \pm b g(t) } = a \lap{f(t)} \pm b \lap{g(t)}.
				</me>
			</p>
		</assemblage>
		
		<p>
			Let's look at an example to see this in action.
		</p>

		<example><title> Transforming a Sum of Functions </title>
			<p>
				<statement>
					<p>
						Compute the Laplace transform
						<me> 
							\lap{15 + 6e^{7t} - 11t}
						</me>.
					</p>
				</statement>
			</p>
			
			<solution>
				<p>
					We apply the linearity property to break the transform down as follows:
					<md>
						<mrow>
							\lap{15 + 6e^{7t} - 11t}
							\amp = \lap{15} + \lap{6e^{7t}} - \lap{11t}
						</mrow>
						<mrow>
							\amp = 15\lap{1} + 6\lap{e^{7t}} - 11\lap{t}
						</mrow>
					</md>
				</p>

				<p>
					Then, using the common transforms, <xref ref="L1" text="title"/>, <xref ref="L2" text="title"/> (<m>a=7</m>), and <xref ref="L3" text="title"/> (<m>n=1</m>), we get 
				</p>

				<md>
					<mrow>
						\lap{15 + 6e^{7t} - 11t}
						\amp = 15\cdot \os{\large s\gt0}{\vphantom{\int^{|}}\frac{1}{s}} + 6\cdot \os{\large s\gt7}{\vphantom{\int^{|}}\frac{1}{s-7}} - 11\cdot \os{\large s\gt0}{\vphantom{\int^{|}}\frac{1}{s^2}}
					</mrow>
					<mrow>
						\amp = \frac{15}{s} + \frac{6}{s-7} - \frac{11}{s^2}, \quad s \gt 7
					</mrow>.
				</md>
				<p>
					We use <m>s \gt 7</m>, because it guarantees that <m>s \gt 0</m> is also true.
				</p>
			</solution>
		</example>

		<task label="chkpt-21"><title> Invalid Uses of Linearity </title>
			<statement>
				<p>
					Which of the following are NOT correct applications of the linearity property?
				</p>
			</statement>
			<choices randomize="yes">
				<choice>
					<statement><m>\lap{4f(t) + 3g(t)} = 4\lap{f(t)} + 3\lap{g(t)}</m></statement>
					<feedback>
						<p>
							No, this is a correct use of the linearity property.
						</p>
					</feedback>
				</choice>
				<choice correct="yes">
					<statement><m>\lap{4f(t) \cdot g(t)} = 4\lap{f(t)} \cdot \lap{g(t)}</m></statement>
					<feedback>
						<p>
							Correct! Linearity does not apply to products of functions, only sums and scalar multiples.
						</p>
					</feedback>
				</choice>
				<choice>
					<statement><m>\lap{5f(t) - 2} = 5\lap{f(t)} - \lap{2}</m></statement>
					<feedback>
						<p>
							No, this is a correct application, linearity applies to differences and constants as well.
						</p>
					</feedback>
				</choice>
				<choice correct="yes">
					<statement><m>\lap{4 f(t)^2} = 4 \lap{f(t)}^2</m></statement>
					<feedback>
						<p>
							Correct! The linearity property does not extend to powers of functions.
						</p>
					</feedback>
				</choice>
			</choices>
		</task>
		
		<task label="chkpt-22"><title> Transform of <m>7\cos t + 2</m> </title>
			<statement>
				<p>
					<m>\ds\lap{7\cos t + 2} = </m> <fillin characters="10" />
				</p>
			</statement>
			<choices randomize="yes">
				<choice correct="yes">
					<statement><m>\ds\frac{7s}{s^2+1} + \frac{2}{s}</m></statement>
					<feedback>
						<p>
							Correct! Use linearity: transform each term individually and add them together.
						</p>
					</feedback>
				</choice>
				<choice>
					<statement><m>\ds\frac{7}{s^2+1} - \frac{2}{s}</m></statement>
					<feedback>
						<p>
							No, the transform of <m>\cos t</m> includes <m>s</m> in the numerator, and the constant should be added.
						</p>
					</feedback>
				</choice>
				<choice>
					<statement><m>\ds\frac{7}{s^2+49} + \frac{2}{s^2}</m></statement>
					<feedback>
						<p>
							No, the frequency of <m>\cos t</m> is 1, not 7, and the constant term transforms to <m>\frac{2}{s}</m>.
						</p>
					</feedback>
				</choice>
				<choice>
					<statement><m>\ds\frac{7s}{s^2+1} - \frac{2}{s^2}</m></statement>
					<feedback>
						<p>
							No, the constant term should yield <m>\frac{2}{s}</m>, and it should be added.
						</p>
					</feedback>
				</choice>
			</choices>
		</task>
	</subsubsection>

	<subsubsection><title> Multiplication by <m>e^{at}</m> (Exponential Shifting) </title>
		<p>
			The translation property, also known as the first shifting theorem, allows us to handle functions multiplied by an exponential term, <m>e^{at}</m>. This property is particularly useful for simplifying the Laplace transforms of products of exponential functions and other functions, such as sine, cosine, or polynomials.
		</p>

		<assemblage xml:id="lt-exp-shifting-property"><title> ‚ú≥Ô∏è Laplace Transform Property: Exponential Shifting </title>
			<p>
				Let <m>F(s) = \lap{f(t)}</m>. Then the Laplace transform of <m>e^{at} f(t)</m> is given by
				<me>
					\lap{ e^{at} f(t) } = F(s-a), \quad a \text{ is a constant}.
				</me>
			</p>
		</assemblage>

		<example><title> Laplace Transform of an Exponential Function </title>
			<p>
				<statement>
					<p>
						Use the exponential shifting property to compute the following
						<ol>
							<li>
								<p> 
									<m>\ds\lap{e^{7t}\cos(3t)}</m>
								</p>
							</li>
							<li>
								<p> 
									<m>\ds\lap{e^{-1.3t}\sin(\pi t)}</m>
								</p>
							</li>
							<li>
								<p> 
									<m>\ds\lap{e^{2t}t^3}</m>
								</p>
							</li>
						</ol>
					</p>
				</statement>
			</p>
			
			<p>				
				<solution>
					<p>
						To apply the <xref ref="lt-exp-shifting-property" text="custom">shifting property</xref>, we identify <m>a=7</m> and <m>f(t)</m> as follows:
						<me>
							\laplacesym \Big\{ \us{\large e^{at}}{\ul{e^{7t}\vphantom{()}}}\ \us{\large f(t)}{\ul{\cos(3t)}} \Big\} = F(s-7), \quad s \gt 7
						</me>
					</p>

					<p>
						By definition, <m>F(s) = \lap{ f(t) }</m>, so 
						<me>
							F(s) = \lap{ \cos(3t) } = \frac{s}{s^2 + 9}
						</me>.
					</p>
					<p>
						Finally, substituting <m>s-7</m> for <m>s</m> in the formula for <m>F(s)</m>, we have
						<me>
							\lap{ e^{7t} \cos(3t) } = F(s-7) = \frac{s-7}{(s-7)^2 + 9}, \quad s \gt 7
						</me>.
					</p>
				</solution>

				<solution>
					<p>
						In this case, <m>a=-1.3</m> and <m>f(t)=\sin(\pi t)</m> since
						<me>
							\laplacesym \Big\{ \us{\large e^{at}}{\ul{e^{-1.3t}\vphantom{()}}}\ \us{\large f(t)}{\ul{\sin(\pi t)}} \Big\} = F(s-(-1.3)) = F(s+1.3), \quad s \gt -1.3
						</me>
					</p>

					<p>
						By definition, <m>F(s) = \lap{ f(t) }</m>, so 
						<me>
							F(s) = \lap{ \sin(\pi t) } = \frac{\pi}{s^2 + \pi^2}
						</me>.
					</p>

					<p>
						Finally, substituting <m>s+1.3</m> for <m>s</m> in the formula for <m>F(s)</m>, we have
						<me>
							\lap{ e^{-1.3t} \sin(\pi t) } = F(s+1.3) = \frac{\pi}{(s+1.3)^2 + \pi^2}, \quad s \gt -1.3
						</me>.
					</p>
				</solution>
				
				<solution>
					<p>
						In this case, <m>a=2</m> and <m>f(t)=t^3</m> since
						<me>
							\laplacesym \Big\{ \us{\large e^{at}}{\ul{e^{2t}\vphantom{()}}}\ \us{\large f(t)}{\ul{t^3\vphantom{()}}} \Big\} = F(s-2), \quad s \gt 2
						</me>
					</p>

					<p>
						By definition, <m>F(s) = \lap{ f(t) }</m>, so 
						<me>
							F(s) = \lap{ t^3 } = \frac{3!}{s^4} = \frac{6}{s^4}
						</me>.
					</p>
					
					<p>
						Finally, substituting <m>s-2</m> for <m>s</m> in the formula for <m>F(s)</m>, we have
						<me>
							\lap{ e^{2t} \sin(\pi t) } = F(s-2) = \frac{6}{(s-2)^4}, \quad s \gt 2
						</me>.
					</p>
				</solution>
			</p>
		</example>

		<task label="chkpt-23"><title> Transform of <m>e^{5t} \sin(2t)</m> </title>
			<statement>
				<p>
					<m>\quad \lap{e^{5t} \sin(2t)} = </m> <fillin characters="15" />
				</p>
			</statement>
			<choices randomize="yes">
				<choice correct="yes">
					<statement><m>\ds\frac{2}{(s-5)^2+4}</m></statement>
					<feedback>
						<p>
							Correct! The translation property shifts the transform of <m>\sin(2t)</m> by 5, giving <m>\ds\frac{2}{(s - 5)^2 + 4}</m>.
						</p>
					</feedback>
				</choice>
				<choice>
					<statement><m>\ds\frac{5}{(s-2)^2+4}</m></statement>
					<feedback>
						<p>
							No, the frequency is 2, not 5, and the shift should be by 5, not 2.
						</p>
					</feedback>
				</choice>
				<choice>
					<statement><m>\ds\frac{2}{(s+5)^2+4}</m></statement>
					<feedback>
						<p>
							No, the shift should be <m>s - 5</m>, not <m>s + 5</m>.
						</p>
					</feedback>
				</choice>
				<choice>
					<statement><m>\ds\frac{5}{(s-2)^2+2}</m></statement>
					<feedback>
						<p>
							No, this has both the wrong frequency and an incorrect value in the denominator.
						</p>
					</feedback>
				</choice>
			</choices>
		</task>
		
		<task label="chkpt-24"><title> Transform of <m>e^{2t} t^3</m> </title>
			<statement>
				<p>
					<m>\quad \lap{e^{2t} t^3} = </m> <fillin characters="15" />
				</p>
			</statement>
			<choices randomize="yes">
				<choice correct="yes">
					<statement><m>6/(s-2)^4</m></statement>
					<feedback>
						<p>
							Correct! First use the rule <m>\lap{t^3} = 6/s^4</m>, then apply the translation property to shift <m>s</m> to <m>s - 2</m>.
						</p>
					</feedback>
				</choice>
				<choice>
					<statement><m>6/(s+2)^4</m></statement>
					<feedback>
						<p>
							No, the correct shift is <m>s - 2</m>, not <m>s + 2</m>.
						</p>
					</feedback>
				</choice>
				<choice>
					<statement><m>3/(s-2)^3</m></statement>
					<feedback>
						<p>
							No, check both the coefficient and exponent, <m>t^3</m> leads to <m>6</m> in the numerator and <m>s^4</m> in the denominator.
						</p>
					</feedback>
				</choice>
				<choice>
					<statement><m>3/(s+2)^3</m></statement>
					<feedback>
						<p>
							No, this expression is incorrect both in sign and power.
						</p>
					</feedback>
				</choice>
			</choices>
		</task>
		
		<task label="chkpt-25"><title> Transform of <m>e^{4t} \cos(5t)</m> </title>
			<statement>
				<p>
					<m>\quad \lap{e^{4t} \cos(5t)} = </m> <fillin characters="15" />
				</p>
			</statement>
			<choices randomize="yes">
				<choice correct="yes">
					<statement><m> \ds\frac{s - 4}{(s - 4)^2 + 25} </m></statement>
					<feedback>
						<p>
							Correct! The Laplace transform of <m>\cos(5t)</m> is <m>\ds\frac{s}{s^2 + 25}</m>, and the translation property shifts <m>s</m> to <m>s - 4</m>.
						</p>
					</feedback>
				</choice>
				<choice>
					<statement><m>4/(s+4)^2+25</m></statement>
					<feedback>
						<p>
							No, the shift should be <m>s - 4</m>. This answer incorrectly uses <m>s + 4</m>.
						</p>
					</feedback>
				</choice>
				<choice>
					<statement><m>5/(s-5)^2+16</m></statement>
					<feedback>
						<p>
							No, the cosine frequency is 5, but the exponential shift is <m>e^{4t}</m>, not <m>e^{5t}</m>. Also, <m>16</m> is <m>4^2</m>, not <m>5^2</m>.
						</p>
					</feedback>
				</choice>
				<choice>
					<statement><m>4/(s-4)^2+16</m></statement>
					<feedback>
						<p>
							No, the correct frequency is 5, so the denominator should be <m>(s - 4)^2 + 25</m>.
						</p>
					</feedback>
				</choice>
			</choices>
		</task>

		<p>
			By applying this property to the functions <m>t^n</m>, <m>\cos(bt)</m>, and <m>\sin(bt)</m>, we can derive additional common Laplace transforms:
		</p>

		<example><title> New Formulas from Exponential Shifting </title>
			<p>
				<statement>
					<p>
						Use the exponential shifting property to derive general formulas for
						<ol>
							<li>
								<p>
									<m>\ds \lap{e^{at}\cos(bt)}</m>
								</p>
							</li>
							<li>
								<p>
									<m>\ds \lap{e^{at}\sin(bt)}</m>
								</p>
							</li>
							<li>
								<p>
									<m>\ds \lap{e^{at}t^n}</m>
								</p>
							</li>
						</ol>
					</p>
				</statement>
			</p>
			<p>
				<solution>
					<p>
						There is not much difference between these and the previous example. 
						<ol>
							<li>
								<p>
									For <m>\lap{e^{at}\cos(bt)}</m>, <m>f(t)=\cos(bt)</m> and
									<md>
										<mrow>					\amp F(s) = \lap{ \cos(bt) } = \frac{s}{s^2 + b^2}			</mrow>
										<mrow>	\implies \quad	\amp F(s-a) = \frac{s-a}{(s-a)^2 + b^2}, \qquad	\text{so,}	</mrow>
									</md>
									<me>
										\boxed{\lap{ e^{at}\cos(bt) } = \frac{s-a}{(s-a)^2 + b^2}, \quad s \gt a}
									</me>
								</p>
							</li>
							<li>
								<p>
									For <m>\lap{e^{at}\sin(bt)}</m>, <m>f(t)=\sin(bt)</m> and
									<md>
										<mrow>					\amp F(s) = \lap{ \sin(bt) } = \frac{b}{s^2 + b^2}			</mrow>
										<mrow>	\implies \quad	\amp F(s-a) = \frac{b}{(s-a)^2 + b^2}, \qquad \text{so,}	</mrow>
									</md>
									<me>
										\boxed{\lap{ e^{at}\sin(bt) } = \frac{b}{(s-a)^2 + b^2}, \quad s \gt a}
									</me>
								</p>
							</li>
							<li>
								<p>
									For <m>\lap{e^{at}t^n}</m>, <m>f(t)=t^n</m> and
									<md>
										<mrow>					\amp F(s) = \lap{ t^n } = \frac{n!}{s^{n+1}}			</mrow>
										<mrow>	\implies \quad	\amp F(s-a) = \frac{n!}{(s-a)^{n+1}}, \qquad \text{so,}	</mrow>
									</md>
									<me>
										\boxed{\lap{ e^{at}t^n } = \frac{n!}{(s-a)^{n+1}}, \quad s \gt a}
									</me>
								</p>
							</li>
						</ol>
					</p>
				</solution>
			</p>
		</example>

		<task label="chkpt-27"><title> Shifted Transform of <m>e^{2t}f(t)</m> </title>
			<statement>
				<p>
					If <m>\ds\lap{f(t)} = \frac{1}{s(s+1)}</m>, what is the Laplace transform of <m>e^{2t}f(t)</m>?
				</p>
			</statement>
			<choices randomize="yes">
				<choice>
					<statement><m>\ds\frac{1}{(s+2)(s+1)}</m></statement>
					<feedback>
						<p>
							No, the translation property requires replacing <m>s</m> with <m>s - 2</m>, not <m>s + 2</m>.
						</p>
					</feedback>
				</choice>
				<choice>
					<statement><m>\ds\frac{1}{s(s+2)}</m></statement>
					<feedback>
						<p>
							No, the shift must be applied to every instance of <m>s</m> in the original transform.
						</p>
					</feedback>
				</choice>
				<choice>
					<statement><m>\ds\frac{1}{(s-2)(s+2)}</m></statement>
					<feedback>
						<p>
							No, the original expression has <m>s</m> and <m>s+1</m>. Shift both to <m>s - 2</m> and <m>s - 1</m> respectively.
						</p>
					</feedback>
				</choice>
				<choice correct="yes">
					<statement><m>\ds\frac{1}{(s-2)(s-1)}</m></statement>
					<feedback>
						<p>
							Correct! Apply the translation property by shifting each <m>s</m> in the original transform to <m>s - 2</m>.
						</p>
					</feedback>
				</choice>
			</choices>
		</task>
	</subsubsection>

	<subsubsection><title> Transforms of Derivatives (Derivative Transfer) </title>
		<p>
			The derivative property of the Laplace transform is one of its most powerful aspects, especially when solving differential equations. This property allows us to transform differential equations into algebraic equations, making them easier to solve.
		</p>

		<assemblage xml:id="lt-derivative-transfer-property"><title> ‚ú≥Ô∏è Laplace Transform Property: Derivative Transfer </title>
			<p>
				Let <m>F(s) = \lap{ f(t) }</m>. If the Laplace transforms of <m>f'(t)</m> exists, then
				<md>
					<mrow>	\lap{ f'(t) }	\amp = s\lap{ f(t) } - f(0)			</mrow>
					<mrow>					\amp \quad\text{ or }				</mrow>
					<mrow>	\lap{ f'(t) }	\amp = sF(s) - f(0), \qquad s \gt 0	</mrow>
				</md>.
			</p>
		</assemblage>

		<theorem xml:id="lt-derivative-transfer-details"><title> Derivative Transfer Property Derivation </title>
			<statement>
				<p>
					To show that
					<me>
						\lap{f'(t)} = s\lap{f(t)} - f(0), \quad s \gt 0
					</me>,
					we start with the definition of the Laplace transform:
				</p>

				<p>
					<me>
						\lap{ f'(t) } = \lim_{b \to \infty} \ub{\int_0^b e^{-st} \cdot f'(t)\ dt}_{I}
					</me>.
				</p>

				<p>
					Applying integration by parts to <m>I</m> once in <m>\lap{ f'(t) }</m> with
					<md>
						<mrow> u = e^{-st},			\quad\amp	dv = f'(t)\ dt,	</mrow>
						<mrow> du = -se^{-st}\ dt,	\quad\amp	v = f(t)		</mrow>
					</md>
					integration by parts gives
					<md>
						<mrow>
							\lap{ t^{n} }
							\amp = \lim_{b \to \infty} \left[f(t)e^{-sb}\Bigg|_0^b - \int_0^b f(t)\cdot\left(-se^{-st}\right)\ dt\right]
						</mrow>
						<mrow>
							\amp = \lim_{b \to \infty} \left[\left(f(b)e^{-sb} - f(0)e^{0}\right) + s\int_0^b e^{-st}\cdot f(t)\ dt\right]
						</mrow>
						<mrow>
							\amp = \lim_{b \to \infty} \left[f(b)e^{-sb} - f(0) + s\int_0^b e^{-st}\cdot f(t)\ dt\right]
						</mrow>
						<mrow>
							\amp = \lim_{b \to \infty} \left[f(b)e^{-sb}\right] - f(0) +  s\lim_{b \to \infty} \left[\int_0^b e^{-st}\cdot f(t)\ dt\right]
						</mrow>
						<mrow>
							\amp = \lim_{b \to \infty} \left[f(b)e^{-sb}\right] - f(0) +  s\ub{\int_\infty^b e^{-st}\cdot f(t)\ dt}_{\large\lap{f(t)}}
						</mrow>.
					</md>
				</p>

				<p>
					Recall that a requirement for the Laplace transform of <m>f(t)</m> to exist is that
					<me>
						\lim_{b \to \infty} \left[f(b)e^{-sb}\right] = 0, \quad s \gt 0
					</me>.
				</p>

				<p>
					Thus, setting the limit to zero gives us the desired result
					<me>
						\lap{ f'(t) } = s\lap{f(t)} - f(0)
					</me>.
				</p>
			</statement>
		</theorem>

		<task label="chkpt-28"><title> Transform of <m>f'(t)</m> with Incorrect Sign </title>
			<statement correct="no">
				<p>
					The Laplace transform of the derivative <m>f'(t)</m> is given by <m>s\lap{f(t)} + f(0)</m>.
				</p>
			</statement>
			<feedback>
				<p>
					False. The correct formula is <m>s\lap{f(t)} - f(0)</m>.
				</p>
			</feedback>
		</task>
		
		<task label="chkpt-29"><title> Derivative Property Converts to Algebra </title>
			<statement correct="yes">
				<p>
					The derivative property of the Laplace transform allows us to convert differential equations into algebraic equations.
				</p>
			</statement>
			<feedback>
				<p>
					True. The derivative property simplifies differential equations into algebraic equations by transforming derivatives into powers of <m>s</m>.
				</p>
			</feedback>
		</task>

		<p>
			This result is crucial for solving differential equations. Essentially, it allows us to eliminate derivatives from an equation, transforming the problem into an algebraic form that's easier to solve. But there's more, this property also works recursively, allowing us to handle higher-order derivatives as well.
		</p>

		<p>
			For example, you can see how this property extends to higher derivatives:
			<md>
				<mrow>
					\lap{ f'(t) }	
					\amp = \us{\large\downarrow}{\ul{\color{green} sF(s) - f(0)}}
				</mrow>
				<mrow>
					\lap{ f''(t) }
					\amp = s {\color{green}\lap{f'(t)}} - f'(0) = s \Big({\color{green}sF(s) - f(0)}\Big) - f'(0)
				</mrow>
				<mrow>
					\amp = \us{\large\downarrow \hspace{5em}}{\ul{\color{blue}s^2F(s) - sf(0) - f'(0)}}
				</mrow>
				<mrow>
					\lap{ f'''(t) }	
					\amp = s {\color{blue}\lap{f''(t)}} - f''(0) = s \Big({\color{blue}s^2F(s) - sf(0) - f'(0)}\Big) - f''(0)
				</mrow>
				<mrow>
					\amp = s^3F(s) - s^2f(0) - sf'(0) - f''(0)
				</mrow>
				<mrow> \vdots </mrow>
				<mrow> 
					\text{and the }
					\amp \text{pattern continues.}
				</mrow>
			</md>
		</p>

		<task label="chkpt-30"><title> Transform of the Derivative of <m>e^t\cos(t)</m> </title>
			<statement>
				<p>
					What value goes in the blank?
					<me>
						\lap{\frac{d}{dt}[e^t\cos (t)]} = s \lap{e^t\cos (t)} - <fillin characters="1" />
					</me>
				</p>
			</statement>
			<choices randomize="yes">
				<choice>
					<statement><m>-1</m></statement>

					</feedback>
				</choice>
				<choice>
					<statement><m>0</m></statement>
					<feedback>
						<p>No, think about which property is being used here.</p>
					</feedback>
				</choice>
				<choice correct="yes">
					<statement><m>1</m></statement>
					<feedback>
						<p>
							Correct!
							<md>
								<mrow>	\lap{\frac{d}{dt}[e^t\cos(t)]}	\amp = s\lap{e^t\cos(t)} - e^0\cos(0)	</mrow>
								<mrow>									\amp = s\lap{e^t\cos(t)} - 1			</mrow>
							</md>
						</p>
					</feedback>
				</choice>
				<choice>
					<statement><m>\pi</m></statement>
					<feedback>
						<p>No, think about which property is being used here.</p>
					</feedback>
				</choice>
			</choices>
		</task>
		
		<task label="chkpt-31"><title> Transform of <m>y''</m> with Given Initial Conditions </title>
			<statement>
				<p>
					If <m>y(0) = 0</m> and <m>y'(0) = 1</m>, then <m>\ds\lap{y''} = s^2Y - <fillin characters="1"/></m>.
				</p>
			</statement>
			<choices randomize="yes">
				<choice correct="yes">
					<statement>1</statement>
					<feedback>
						<p>Correct! The Laplace transform of <m>y''</m> is <m>s^2Y - sy(0) - y'(0)</m>. Since <m>y(0) = 0</m> and <m>y'(0) = 1</m>, this simplifies to <m>s^2Y - 1</m>.</p>
					</feedback>
				</choice>
				<choice>
					<statement>0</statement>
					<feedback>
						<p>No, you must subtract <m>y'(0)</m>, which equals 1.</p>
					</feedback>
				</choice>
				<choice>
					<statement>s</statement>
					<feedback>
						<p>No, the subtraction term is <m>1</m>, not <m>s</m>.</p>
					</feedback>
				</choice>
				<choice>
					<statement>-1</statement>
					<feedback>
						<p>No, the sign should be negative already in the formula; the constant subtracted is <m>+1</m>.</p>
					</feedback>
				</choice>
			</choices>
		</task>
		
		<task label="chkpt-32"><title> Transform of <m>y'(t)</m> When <m>y(0) = 0</m> </title>
			<statement>
				<p>
					What is the correct Laplace transform of <m>y'(t)</m> given that <m>y(0) = 0</m>?
				</p>
			</statement>
			<choices randomize="yes">
				<choice>

					</feedback>
				</choice>
				<choice>
					<statement><m>Y(s)</m></statement>
					<feedback>
						<p>No, this lacks the factor of <m>s</m> introduced by the derivative.</p>
					</feedback>
				</choice>

					<statement><m>sY(s)</m></statement>

						<p>Correct! The Laplace transform of <m>y'(t)</m> with <m>y(0) = 0</m> is simply <m>sY(s)</m>.</p>
					</feedback>
				</choice>
				<choice>
					<statement><m>sY(s) - 1</m></statement>
					<feedback>
						<p>No, there's no need to subtract anything here since <m>y(0) = 0</m>.</p>
					</feedback>
				</choice>
			</choices>
		</task>
		
		<task label="chkpt-33"><title> Transform of <m>y''</m> Using General Formula </title>
			<statement>
				<p>
					The Laplace transform of the second derivative <m>y''</m> is given by <m>s^2Y - <fillin characters="3"/></m>.
				</p>
			</statement>

				<choice correct="yes">

					</feedback>
				</choice>
				<choice>

					</feedback>
				</choice>
				<choice>

			</choices>
		</task>
	</subsubsection>

	<subsubsection><title> Multiplication by <m>t^n</m> (Laplace Derivatives) </title>
		<p>
			The final property we'll explore is how the Laplace transform handles functions multiplied by a power of <m>t</m>. This property is especially useful when dealing with polynomial terms in differential equations.
		</p>

		<assemblage xml:id="lt-laplace-derivative-property"><title> ‚ú≥Ô∏è Laplace Transform Property: Laplace Derivatives </title>
			<p>
				Let <m>F(s) = \lap{ f(t) }</m>. Then we have the following property:
				<md>
					<mrow>
						\lap{t^n f(t)}
						\amp = (-1)^n \frac{d^n}{ds^n}\Big[\lap{f(t)}\Big]
					</mrow>
					<mrow>
						\amp \quad\text{ or }
					</mrow>
					<mrow>
						\lap{t^n f(t)}	
						\amp = (-1)^n \frac{d^n}{ds^n}\Big[F(s)\Big],\quad s &gt; 0,\ n = 1, 2, 3, \ldots
					</mrow>
				</md>
			</p>
		</assemblage>

		<theorem xml:id="lt-laplace-derivatives-details"><title> Derivative Transfer Property Derivation </title>
			<statement>
				<p>
					To show that
					<me>
						\lap{t^n f(t)} = (-1)^n \frac{d^n}{ds^n}\left[\lap{f(t)}\right]
					</me>,
					we start on the right side and show that it leads to the left side.
				</p>

				<p>
					<md>
						<mrow>
							(-1)^n \frac{d^n}{ds^n}\left[\lap{f(t)}\right]
							\amp = (-1)^n \frac{d^n}{ds^n}\left[\int_0^{\infty} e^{-st} f(t)\ dt\right]
						</mrow>
						<mrow>
							\amp = (-1)^n \int_0^{\infty} \frac{\partial^n}{\partial s^n}\left[e^{-st} f(t)\right]\ dt
						</mrow>
						<mrow>
							\amp = (-1)^n \int_0^{\infty} f(t)\frac{\partial^n}{\partial s^n}\left[e^{-st}\right]\ dt
						</mrow>
						<mrow>
							\amp = (-1)^n \int_0^{\infty} f(t) \left((-t)^n e^{-st}\right)\ dt
						</mrow>
						<mrow>
							\amp = (-1)^n \int_0^{\infty} e^{-st} t^n f(t)\ dt
						</mrow>
						<mrow>
							\amp = \int_0^{\infty} e^{-st} t^n f(t)\ dt
						</mrow>
						<mrow>
							\amp = \lap{t^n f(t)}
						</mrow>
					</md>
				</p>
			</statement>
		</theorem>

		<task label="chkpt-34"><title> Derivative Order for <m>\lap{t^2 f(t)}</m> </title>
			<statement>
				<p>
					The Laplace transform of <m>\ds t^2 f(t) </m> is equal to <fillin characters="10" /> derivative of the Laplace transform of <m>\ds f(t) </m> with respect to <m>s</m>.
				</p>
			</statement>
			<choices randomize="yes">
				<choice correct="yes">
					<statement>second</statement>
					<feedback>
						<p>
							Correct! The Laplace transform of <m>t^2 f(t)</m> is the second derivative of <m>\lap{f(t)}</m> with respect to <m>s</m>, multiplied by <m>(-1)^2 = 1</m>.
						</p>
					</feedback>
				</choice>
				<choice>
					<statement>first</statement>
					<feedback>
						<p>
							Incorrect. A first derivative applies to <m>t f(t)</m>, not <m>t^2 f(t)</m>.
						</p>
					</feedback>
				</choice>
				<choice>
					<statement>third</statement>
					<feedback>
						<p>
							Incorrect. Third derivatives arise when transforming <m>t^3 f(t)</m>.
						</p>
					</feedback>
				</choice>
				<choice>
					<statement>fourth</statement>
					<feedback>
						<p>
							Incorrect. <m>t^2 f(t)</m> requires the second derivative, not the fourth.
						</p>
					</feedback>
				</choice>
			</choices>
		</task>
			
		<task label="chkpt-35"><title> Transform of <m>t^3 f(t)</m> via Derivatives </title>
			<statement>
				<p>
					What is the Laplace transform of <m>t^3 f(t)</m> in terms of <m>\lap{f(t)}</m>?
				</p>
			</statement>
			<choices randomize="yes">
				<choice>
					<statement><m>\ds -\frac{d^3}{ds^3} \lap{f(t)}</m></statement>
					<feedback>
						<p>
							Correct! The transform of <m>t^3 f(t)</m> is <m>\ds (-1)^3 \frac{d^3}{ds^3} \lap{f(t)} = -\frac{d^3}{ds^3} \lap{f(t)}</m>.
						</p>
					</feedback>
				</choice>
				<choice>
					<statement><m>\ds \frac{d^3}{ds^3} \lap{f(t)}</m></statement>
					<feedback>
						<p>
							Incorrect. You're missing the factor of <m>-1</m> from <m>(-1)^3</m>.
						</p>
					</feedback>
				</choice>
				<choice>
					<statement><m>\ds -\frac{d^2}{ds^2} \lap{f(t)}</m></statement>
					<feedback>
						<p>
							Incorrect. That would be the result for <m>t^2 f(t)</m>, not <m>t^3 f(t)</m>.
						</p>
					</feedback>
				</choice>
				<choice>
					<statement><m>\ds \frac{d^2}{ds^2} \lap{f(t)}</m></statement>
					<feedback>
						<p>
							Incorrect. You need a third derivative and a negative sign.
						</p>
					</feedback>
				</choice>
			</choices>
		</task>
		
		<task label="chkpt-36"><title> Apply Derivative Rule to <m>\lap{tf(t)}</m> </title>
			<statement>
				<p>
					Hypothetically, if <m>\lap{f(t)} = \cos(2s)</m>, then <m>\lap{tf(t)} = </m> <fillin characters="10" />
				</p>
			</statement>
			<choices randomize="yes">
				<choice>
					<statement><m>\ds -2\sin(2s)</m></statement>
					<feedback>
						<p>
							Incorrect. You forgot to apply the negative from the derivative rule properly.
						</p>
					</feedback>
				</choice>
				<choice correct="yes">
					<statement><m>\ds 2\sin(2s)</m></statement>
					<feedback>
						<p>
							Correct!
							<md>
								<mrow>	\lap{tf(t)} \amp = -\frac{d}{ds}[\lap{f(t)}] </mrow>
								<mrow>				\amp = -\frac{d}{ds}[\cos(2s)] </mrow>
								<mrow>				\amp = -(-2\sin(2s)) </mrow>
								<mrow>				\amp = 2\sin(2s) </mrow>
							</md>
						</p>
					</feedback>
				</choice>
				<choice>
					<statement><m>\ds -\sin(2s) + 2\cos(2s)</m></statement>
					<feedback>
						<p>
							Incorrect. The Laplace transform of <m>tf(t)</m> should be the negative derivative of <m>\cos(2s)</m>, not this combination.
						</p>
					</feedback>
				</choice>
				<choice>
					<statement><m>\ds 2\sin(2s) + \cos(2s)</m></statement>
					<feedback>
						<p>
							Incorrect. The rule gives only a sine term, no cosine remains after differentiation.
						</p>
					</feedback>
				</choice>
			</choices>
		</task>

		<example>
			<p>
				<statement>
					<p>
						Use the <xref ref="lt-laplace-derivative-property" text="custom">Laplace derivative property</xref> to compute
						<ol>
							<li>
								<p>
									<m>\ds\lap{t \cos(t)}</m>
								</p>
							</li>
							<li>
								<p>
									<m>\ds\lap{t^2 \sin(-5t)}</m>
								</p>
							</li>
						</ol>
					</p>
				</statement>
			</p>
			
			<p>
				<solution>
					<p>
						To apply the <xref ref="lt-laplace-derivative-property" text="custom">Laplace derivative property</xref>, we identify <m>n=1</m> and <m>f(t)</m>:
						<me>
							\laplacesym \Big\{ t^1 \ \us{\large f(t)}{\ul{\cos(t)}} \Big\} = (-1)^1 F'(s) = -F'(s), \quad s \gt 0
						</me>
					</p>

					<p>
						By definition, <m>F(s) = \lap{ f(t) }</m>, so 
						<me>
							F(s) = \lap{ \cos(t) } = \frac{s}{s^2 + 1}
						</me>
						and the quotient rule gives us
						<me>
							F'(s) = \frac{(s^2 + 1)(1) - s(2s)}{(s^2 + 1)^2} = \frac{1 - s^2}{(s^2 + 1)^2}
						</me>
					</p>
					<p>
						Therefore,
						<me>
							\lap{t \cos(t)} = -\frac{1 - s^2}{(s^2 + 1)^2} = \frac{s^2 - 1}{(s^2 + 1)^2}, \quad s \gt 0
						</me>.
					</p>
				</solution>

				<solution>
					<p>
						In this case, <m>n=2</m> and <m>f(t) = \sin(-5t)</m>, so 
						<me>
							\laplacesym \Big\{ t^2 \ \us{\large f(t)}{\ul{\sin(-5t)}} \Big\} = (-1)^2 F''(s) = F''(s), \quad s \gt 0
						</me>
					</p>

					<p>
						By definition, <m>F(s) = \lap{ f(t) }</m>, so 
						<me>
							F(s) = \lap{ \sin(-5t) } = \frac{-5}{s^2 + 25} = -5(s^2 + 25)^{-1}
						</me>
						and two derivatives of this function gives us
						<md>
							<mrow>	F'(s)	\amp = \frac{d}{ds}\left[-5(s^2 + 25)^{-1}\right]				</mrow>
							<mrow>			\amp = -5\left(-1\right)(s^2 + 25)^{-2}(2s)						</mrow>
							<mrow>			\amp = 10s(s^2 + 25)^{-2}										</mrow>
							<mrow>	F''(s)	\amp = \frac{d}{ds}\left[10s(s^2 + 25)^{-2}\right]				</mrow>
							<mrow>			\amp = 10(s^2 + 25)^{-2} + 10s(-2)(s^2 + 25)^{-3}(2s)			</mrow>
							<mrow>			\amp = \frac{10}{(s^2 + 25)^{2}} - \frac{40s^2}{(s^2 + 25)^{3}}	</mrow>
						</md>
					</p>
					<p>
						Therefore,
						<me>
							\lap{t^2 \sin(-5t)} = \frac{10}{(s^2 + 25)^{2}} - \frac{40s^2}{(s^2 + 25)^{3}}, \quad s \gt 0
						</me>.
					</p>
				</solution>
			</p>
		</example>
	
		<p>
			This shows that multiplying a function by <m>t</m> inside a Laplace transform is equivalent to taking the derivative of the Laplace transform of the same function, multiplied by <m>-1</m>. It turns out that each additional power of <m>t</m> adds another negative sign and derivative.
		</p>
	</subsubsection>
</subsection>

<subsection><title> Table of Laplace Transforms </title>
	<lemma><title> üëÄ <em>Reading Preparation</em> </title>
		<p>
	
			Needs to be completed.
			
		</p>
	</lemma>

	<p>
		We have done a lot of work computing common transforms and deriving the properties of the Laplace transform. Now, we can summarize all of this information in a table for quick reference.
	</p>

	<assemblage xml:id="lt-table-L1-L12-shit"><title> ‚ú≥Ô∏è Common Laplace Transforms and Properties </title>
		<p>
			Assume <m>F(s) = \lap{f(t)}</m>, <m>a</m>, <m>b</m> are constant, and <m>n=0,1,2,3,\ldots</m>
		</p>

		<p>
			<sidebyside widths="5% 17% 50% 8%" margins="10% 10%" valign="middle">
				<p/>
				<p> <me> f(t) </me> </p>
				<p> <me> \lap{f(t)} = F(s) </me> </p>
				<p/>
			</sidebyside>
			<sidebyside widths="5% 17% 50% 8%" margins="10% 10%" valign="middle">
				<p> <me>L_{1}</me> </p>
				<p> <me> 1 </me> </p>
				<p> <me>\frac{1}{s}</me> </p>
				<p> <me>\vphantom{\int}s &gt; 0</me> </p>
			</sidebyside>
			<sidebyside widths="5% 17% 50% 8%" margins="10% 10%" valign="middle">
				<p> <me>L_{2}</me> </p>
				<p> <me> e^{at} </me> </p>
				<p> <me>\frac{1}{s - a}</me> </p>
				<p> <me>\vphantom{\int}s &gt; 0</me> </p>
			</sidebyside>
			<sidebyside widths="5% 17% 50% 8%" margins="10% 10%" valign="middle">
				<p> <me>L_{3}</me> </p>
				<p> <me> t^n </me> </p>
				<p> <me>\frac{n!}{s^{n+1}}</me> </p>
				<p> <me>\vphantom{\int}s &gt; 0</me> </p>
			</sidebyside>
			<sidebyside widths="5% 17% 50% 8%" margins="10% 10%" valign="middle">
				<p> <me>L_{4}</me> </p>
				<p> <me>\sin(bt)</me> </p>
				<p> <me>\frac{b}{s^2+b^2}</me> </p>
				<p> <me>\vphantom{\int}s &gt; 0</me> </p>
			</sidebyside>
			<sidebyside widths="5% 17% 50% 8%" margins="10% 10%" valign="middle">
				<p> <me>L_{5}</me> </p>
				<p> <me>\cos(bt)</me> </p>
				<p> <me>\frac{s}{s^2+b^2}</me> </p>
				<p> <me>\vphantom{\int}s &gt; 0</me> </p>
			</sidebyside>
			<sidebyside widths="5% 17% 50% 8%" margins="10% 10%" valign="middle">
				<p> <me>L_{6}</me> </p>
				<p> <me> e^{at}\ t^n </me> </p>
				<p> <me>\frac{n!}{(s-a)^{n+1}}</me> </p>
				<p> <me>\vphantom{\int}s &gt; a</me> </p>
			</sidebyside>
			<sidebyside widths="5% 17% 50% 8%" margins="10% 10%" valign="middle">
				<p> <me>L_{7}</me> </p>
				<p> <me> e^{at}\sin(bt) </me> </p>
				<p> <me>\frac{b}{(s-a)^2+b^2}</me> </p>
				<p> <me>\vphantom{\int}s &gt; a</me> </p>
			</sidebyside>
			<sidebyside widths="5% 17% 50% 8%" margins="10% 10%" valign="middle">
				<p> <me>L_{8}</me> </p>
				<p> <me> e^{at}\cos(bt) </me> </p>
				<p> <me>\frac{s-a}{(s-a)^2+b^2}</me> </p>
				<p> <me>\vphantom{\int}s &gt; a</me> </p>
			</sidebyside>
			<sidebyside widths="5% 17% 50% 8%" margins="10% 10%" valign="middle">
				<p> <me>L_{9}</me> </p>
				<p> <me> y'(t) </me> </p>
				<p> <me>sY(s) - y(0) </me> </p>
				<p> <me>\vphantom{\int}s &gt; 0</me> </p>
			</sidebyside>
			<sidebyside widths="5% 17% 50% 8%" margins="10% 10%" valign="middle">
				<p> <me>L_{10}</me> </p>
				<p> <me> y''(t) </me> </p>
				<p> <me>s^2Y(s) - sy(0) - y'(0) </me> </p>
				<p> <me>\vphantom{\int}s &gt; 0</me> </p>
			</sidebyside>
			<sidebyside widths="5% 17% 50% 8%" margins="10% 10%" valign="middle">
				<p> <me>L_{11}</me> </p>
				<p> <me> y'''(t) </me> </p>
				<p> <me>s^3Y(s) - s^2y(0) - sy'(0) - y''(0) </me> </p>
				<p> <me>\vphantom{\int}s &gt; 0</me> </p>
			</sidebyside>
			<sidebyside widths="5% 17% 50% 8%" margins="10% 10%" valign="middle">
				<p> <me>L_{12}</me> </p>
				<p> <me> t^n f(t) </me> </p>
				<p> <me>(-1)^n \frac{d^{(n)}}{ds^{(n)}}F(s)</me> </p>
				<p> <me>\vphantom{\int}s &gt; 0</me> </p>
			</sidebyside>
		</p>
	</assemblage>

    <table xml:id="lt-table-L1-L12">
        <tabular top="minor" bottom="minor" left="minor" right="minor" halign="center" width="100%">
            <row bottom="none">
                <cell/>

                <cell><me>e^{at}\sin(bt)</me></cell>
                <cell><me>\frac{b}{(s-a)^2+b^2}</me></cell>
                <cell><me>s &gt; a</me></cell>
            </row>
            <row>
                <cell>L<m>_8</m></cell>
                <cell><me>e^{at}\cos(bt)</me></cell>
                <cell><me>\frac{s-a}{(s-a)^2+b^2}</me></cell>
                <cell><me>s &gt; a</me></cell>
            </row>
            <row>
                <cell>L<m>_9</m></cell>
                <cell><me>y'(t)</me></cell>
                <cell><me>sY(s) - y(0)</me></cell>

            </row>
            <row>
                <cell>L<m>_{10}</m></cell>
                <cell><me>y''(t)</me></cell>
                <cell><me>s^2Y(s) - sy(0) - y'(0)</me></cell>
                <cell><me>s &gt; 0</me></cell>
            </row>
            <row>
                <cell>L<m>_{11}</m></cell>
                <cell><me>y'''(t)</me></cell>
                <cell><me>s^3Y(s) - s^2y(0) - sy'(0) - y''(0)</me></cell>
                <cell><me>s &gt; 0</me></cell>
            </row>
            <row>
                <cell>L<m>_{12}</m></cell>
                <cell><me>t^n f(t)</me></cell>
                <cell><me>(-1)^n \frac{d^{(n)}}{ds^{(n)}}F(s)</me></cell>
                <cell><me>s &gt; 0</me></cell>
            </row>
        </tabular>
    </table>
</subsection>

<introduction>
	<p>
		The heart of the Laplace transform method lies in its ability to convert a differential equation into an algebraic equation. This transformation takes place in what we call the <em>Laplace domain</em>, a mathematical space where derivatives become algebraic expressions and initial conditions are naturally incorporated into the equation.
	</p>

	<p>
		In this section, we will break down how to solve the model equation
		<men xml:id="lt-model-eqn-w-ics"> 
			y'' - 9y = 10e^{2t}, \quad y(0) = 1, \quad y'(0) = -7
		</men>
		using the steps outlined in the <xref ref="lt-roadmap" text="custom">Laplace Transform Roadmap</xref>:
	</p>
		
	<ol marker="(i)">
		<li>
			<p>
				We begin with <xref ref="lt-roadmap-step-1" text="custom">step 1</xref>, where the forward transform moves the differential equation into the Laplace domain.
			</p>
		</li>
		<li>
			<p>
				Then, we jump to <xref ref="lt-roadmap-step-4" text="custom">step 4</xref> to explore how the transformed equation is converted back into the original domain using a backward transform.
			</p>
		</li>
		<li>
			<p>
